<div id="review_484928341" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484928341" />
    <a title="Guillermo  " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11481458-guillermo"><img alt="Guillermo  " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417725408p2/11481458.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/484928341?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Guillermo  " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Guillermo  " href="/user/show/11481458-guillermo">Guillermo  </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer484928341" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3286490762351794633">I was sad when it ended. I'll miss the wonderful characters Vonnegut<br/> has created. But like all of Vonnegut's books, it's one I hope to revisit many times in the future.<br/><br/>Bluebeard is a fictional autobiography of a cranky old<br/> Armenian modern painter living alone on a beachside estate. His life<br/> is forever changed one day when he meets Circe Berman and is pressured<br/> by her to write his autobiography – Bluebeard. We spend our time with<br/> Rabo Karabekian divided between the present day, and the past. The<br/> </span>
  <span id="freeText3286490762351794633" style="display:none">I was sad when it ended. I'll miss the wonderful characters Vonnegut<br /> has created. But like all of Vonnegut's books, it's one I hope to revisit many times in the future.<br /><br />Bluebeard is a fictional autobiography of a cranky old<br /> Armenian modern painter living alone on a beachside estate. His life<br /> is forever changed one day when he meets Circe Berman and is pressured<br /> by her to write his autobiography – Bluebeard. We spend our time with<br /> Rabo Karabekian divided between the present day, and the past. The<br /> hilarity ensures. I read this mostly on a train to and from work, and<br /> must have looked slightly ridiculous with all the times I shut the<br /> book and just laughed. <br /><br />This is a book that deals with the Armenian<br /> genocide, a man that beats up his wife, suicide, being maimed in World<br /> War II (our protagonist was not born a Cyclops, he tells us on page 1,<br /> he was deprived of his eye while commanding a platoon of Army<br /> Engineers), and about the desolation a man feels as he looks back at<br /> all his failures in his life. All this, and the book was laugh out<br /> loud funny, never felt too heavy, and concluded so triumphantly and<br /> hopeful, that it got me slightly (very slightly!) teary eyed. Only<br /> Vonnegut.<br /><br /> Vonnegut has a rare gift I don’t think I’ve ever come across. He just<br /> makes writing look so damn easy. He writes as if he was speaking to a<br /> small child, but it is never ever condescending. It just flows with<br /> such ease, elegance, and efficiency.<br /><br /> The plot of the book isn’t really important. This is a prime example<br /> of substance over form. I’ve read and reread many of Vonnegut’s books<br /> and to this day, if you pinned me down and asked me to recall for you<br /> the plot of Breakfast of Champions or of The Sirens of Titan, I would<br /> fail miserably. You don’t read Vonnegut for plots, you read his work<br /> because of that wonderful dark humor- that voice that cries out about<br /> the absurdity of it all. That being said, I think this would be a<br /> great first book for those not familiar with Vonnegut. It’s probably<br /> the most straight forward Vonnegut novel that I’ve read so far; no<br /> zany aliens or time travel.<br /><br /> The absurdity of war is a note that Vonnegut loves to play. Also, his<br /> disdain for the male sex in general:<br /> "After all that men have done to the women and children and every<br /> other defenseless thing on this planet, it is time that not just every<br /> painting, but every piece of music, every statue, every play, ever<br /> poem and every book a man creates, should say only this: "We are much<br /> too horrible for this nice place. We give up. We quit. The end!"<br /><br /> Being a modern painter, Karobekian recalls his years as a struggling<br /> artist. Having been struggling musician myself, a lot of what Vonnegut<br /> writes about rings a bell of truth for me.<br /> <br />"A moderately gifted person who would have been a community treasure a<br /> thousand years ago has to give up, has to go into some other line of<br /> work, since modern communications put him or her into daily<br /> competition with the world's champions. The entire planet can get<br /> along nicely now with maybe a dozen champion performers in each area<br /> of human giftedness. A moderately gifted person has to keep his or her<br /> gifts all bottled up until, in a manner of speaking, he or she gets<br /> drunk at a wedding and tap dances on the coffee table like Fred<br /> Astaire or Ginger Rogers. We have a name for him or her. We call him<br /> or her an "exhibitionist."<br /> How do we reward such an exhibitionist? We say to him or her next<br /> morning. "Wow! Were you ever drunk last night!"<br /><br />But for such a seemingly light and fun read, his message is<br /> surprisingly deep. Not to simplify things, but war really is absurd,<br /> and the media has done a disservice by glorifying it (although this is<br /> a trend seen less and less with more “realistic” portrayals of war<br /> such as Saving Private Ryan and Born on the Fourth of July). I’ll<br /> conclude this with a final quote from Bluebeard's 245th page:<br /><br /> "All the returning veterans in the movies are our age or older," he<br /> said. That was true. In the movies you seldom saw the babies who had<br /> done most of the heavy fighting on the ground in the war.<br /> "Yes- "I said, "and most of the actors in the movies never even went<br /> to war. They came home to the wife and kids and swimming pool after<br /> every grueling day in front of the cameras, after firing off blank<br /> cartridges while men all around them were spitting catsup."<br /> "That's what the young people will think our war was fifty years from<br /> now," said Kitchen, "old men and blanks and catsup." So they would .<br /> So they do.<br /> "Because of the movies," he predicted, "nobody will believe that it<br /> was babies who fought the war."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3286490762351794633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6916290?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/395947-lindsay">Lindsay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6916290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer956439975760043409">I was lured to this book by Breakfast of Champions, a Vonnegut book that I loved. But sadly I was disappointed. I wanted Vonnegut’s classic writing style; his unpredictable qualms, his interrogative view of the world and his illuminating illustrations. Instead, I received none of that. Bluebeard is unusual in comparison to his other books. Its critiques on the world and human life are blatant and deliberate, rather than his usual subtle remarks. The main character, Rabo Karabekian, is a widowed </span>
  <span id="freeText956439975760043409" style="display:none">I was lured to this book by Breakfast of Champions, a Vonnegut book that I loved. But sadly I was disappointed. I wanted Vonnegut’s classic writing style; his unpredictable qualms, his interrogative view of the world and his illuminating illustrations. Instead, I received none of that. Bluebeard is unusual in comparison to his other books. Its critiques on the world and human life are blatant and deliberate, rather than his usual subtle remarks. The main character, Rabo Karabekian, is a widowed former painter who is writing as an autobiography. (Vonnegut goes so far to make the book Rabo’s, that he credits a fictional character in the dedication). Rabo is a sad character. He lives in a mansion that belonged to his deceased second wife, his only companions are the cook and her daughter (who don’t for the old man much) and crippled war veteran. Rabo himself is missing an eye. When a bossy, mysterious writer invites herself into his mansion, Rabo opens up about his life, his art and his sadness. For me, the story was slow moving, and plodding until the reader discovers the secret in the barn. This saved the book. While the Rabo’s memoirs are completely boring, the suspense Vonnegut builds for Rabo’s secret is well constructed. In all, it was a quick read but it didn’t satisfy its full potential.</span>
  <a data-text-id="956439975760043409" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14780912725875490197" style="display:none">I enjoyed this story. Listening to elder folks tell their story &amp; their perspectives can give insight to a time before mine. <br />Rabo Karabekian is writing is memoir and we, the readers, are in on the story. There's war, childhood, friendships, loss, gain...… there's Life in all it's turmoil. <br />Throughout Rabo is expressing his lament that his paintings, which he considers mediocre, are somewhat outside the realm of "great art". And yet he'd like them to withstand the test of time and remain....but they've been painted with an inferior paint that isn't standing up to time. <br />What is the purpose of art, of life, of himself? Within this story he ponders all this and more. <br />The secret of the Potato Barn?...… read this book to find out. It's fantastic. <br /><br />Listened to this while jogging. Throughout this audiobook, this book reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10503.The_Mysterious_Flame_of_Queen_Loana" title="The Mysterious Flame of Queen Loana by Umberto Eco" rel="nofollow noopener">The Mysterious Flame of Queen Loana</a> by Umberto Eco. Both books brought the elderly protagonists to life as they told their life stories. Both were quietly told stories with lots of depth.</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer539839955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer531895411009400387">I would call this the most mature of any of Vonnegut's books that I have read so far. I know that Vonnegut began his novel writing close to the age of 30 which is considered an adult but his work still lacked maturity. Which can be a good thing as his earlier works were meant to be biting satire and not high literature. <br/><br/>Bluebeard is more melancholy and less slapstick than Slaughterhouse-Five and Breakfast of Champions which he is more renowned for. It has a more subtle humour that lends itself t</span>
  <span id="freeText531895411009400387" style="display:none">I would call this the most mature of any of Vonnegut's books that I have read so far. I know that Vonnegut began his novel writing close to the age of 30 which is considered an adult but his work still lacked maturity. Which can be a good thing as his earlier works were meant to be biting satire and not high literature. <br /><br />Bluebeard is more melancholy and less slapstick than Slaughterhouse-Five and Breakfast of Champions which he is more renowned for. It has a more subtle humour that lends itself to better storytelling. This perspective and style work really well when looking at life through art. I felt I was looking into Vonnegut's heart and mind as I read each page. The medium is different but the message is the same. This really make's McLuhan's <i>the medium is the message</i> resonate with me. That's not to say he doesn't take the occasional bite out of how we view the art world. He does, and with relish.<br /><br />I think I've found a new favourite by Mr. Vonnegut and one I would rather use to introduce people to the real brilliance of his writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="531895411009400387" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6191030" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6191030" />
    <a title="Dave-O" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/189406-dave-o"><img alt="Dave-O" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1243477744p2/189406.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6191030?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dave-O" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dave-O" href="/user/show/189406-dave-o">Dave-O</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fictional Abstract Expressionists</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/189406-dave-o?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6191030" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2947710102906541516">Vonnegut's biting satire comes through with this, his profile of fictional artist Rabo Karabekian. The book spans such events as the Turkish Armenian genocide, World War II, and the post-war climate in New York that gave birth to Abstract-Expressionism. <br/><br/>The genius of Vonnegut is his ability to see the humor in the worst tragedies, all of which he says are born of human folly. The protagonist just wants to live out his last days on his Long Island home but then is convinced by a seductive widow t</span>
  <span id="freeText2947710102906541516" style="display:none">Vonnegut's biting satire comes through with this, his profile of fictional artist Rabo Karabekian. The book spans such events as the Turkish Armenian genocide, World War II, and the post-war climate in New York that gave birth to Abstract-Expressionism. <br /><br />The genius of Vonnegut is his ability to see the humor in the worst tragedies, all of which he says are born of human folly. The protagonist just wants to live out his last days on his Long Island home but then is convinced by a seductive widow to write his own autobiography. Karabekian is the strongest character in the book (the others are not as developed as I would have liked) and through him, Vonnegut's love for art and particularly the Ab-Ex movement come through. An art movement not without its own tragedies for the painters involved. "Survivor guilt" is a theme throughout the book as Karabekian goes back and forth between his own writings and his unfolding uneasy relationship with the Widow. Metaphorically, she represents the shock of the new: a creative jolt he has missed since his second wife's death. The real mystery is whether he will bare his soul to her, along with the secret he keeps locked in his potato barn.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2947710102906541516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1451711417" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1451711417" />
    <a title="Tyler Jones" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2171826-tyler-jones"><img alt="Tyler Jones" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310146343p2/2171826.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1451711417?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tyler Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tyler Jones" href="/user/show/2171826-tyler-jones">Tyler Jones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2171826-tyler-jones?shelf=art">art</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1451711417" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1419671766200990862">Second Reading, May 2020:<br/>I was in my early twenties when I first read this novel. I think young people are particularly drawn to Vonnegut. His anti-establishment attitudes resonated with young punks like me. It is a shame that more people don't read him, or re-read him, when they are older. <i>Bluebeard</i> is a book that the fifty-something me enjoyed even more than the twenty-something me did, which make sense as it is about a man at the end of his life coming to terms with his past. As such, the old</span>
  <span id="freeText1419671766200990862" style="display:none">Second Reading, May 2020:<br />I was in my early twenties when I first read this novel. I think young people are particularly drawn to Vonnegut. His anti-establishment attitudes resonated with young punks like me. It is a shame that more people don't read him, or re-read him, when they are older. <i>Bluebeard</i> is a book that the fifty-something me enjoyed even more than the twenty-something me did, which make sense as it is about a man at the end of his life coming to terms with his past. As such, the older reader might find it more moving than a youngster would.<br /><br />It was jarring to come across the following passage, written in the mid 1980's, which sums up the mentality of people who would make up the MAGA movement three decades later: <br /><br /><i>The darkest secret of this country, I am afraid, is that too many of its citizens imagine that they belong to a much higher civilization somewhere else. That civilization doesn't have to be another country. It can be the past instead - the United States before it was spoiled by immigrants and the enfranchisement of the blacks.</i><br /><br />Re-read this one if, like me, it's been a few decades since your last time, or if you have forgotten how wise cranky old Kurt could be. <br /><br />First Reading, Nov. 1987:<br />I was once a veracious reader of Vonnegut and I have to admit there is some truth to the criticism that is later novels became formulaic. The man himself admitted that writing became more of a chore and less of a joy as the years passed, and I think it shows in books that became increasingly bitter and less vibrant. The exception, in my opinion, is the wonderful <i>Bluebeard</i> which seems to have a sort of light touch and...if you really look hard...optimism? Maybe I go to far, but this story of an artist and the nature art is a departure and a delight. For originality and fun, you can't beat <i>Monkey House</i>, but of his novels I think this comes close.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1419671766200990862" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1035448842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13661136855325022761"><p>It’s an amazing feeling to read your favorite author’s book written about a person of your nationality. 'Bluebeard' is the imaginary autobiography of Rabo Karabekian, an abstract-expressionist painter and close friend of Rothko and Jackson Pollock in post-war New York. Although born in America of immigrant Armenian parents, he is haunted by their close escape from the first genocide of the twentieth century, the massacre by the Turks of a million of their Armenian citizens. Vonnegut did a good r</p></span>
  <span id="freeText13661136855325022761" style="display:none"><p>It’s an amazing feeling to read your favorite author’s book written about a person of your nationality. 'Bluebeard' is the imaginary autobiography of Rabo Karabekian, an abstract-expressionist painter and close friend of Rothko and Jackson Pollock in post-war New York. Although born in America of immigrant Armenian parents, he is haunted by their close escape from the first genocide of the twentieth century, the massacre by the Turks of a million of their Armenian citizens. Vonnegut did a good research about Armenian history and culture in order to make his character and his feelings more realistic. </p><br /><p>If Bluebeard is not one of Vonnegut’s two or three best books, it is nevertheless very much worth the ride. Also there is a considerable feminist theme. Once started, Bluebeard is will be hard to put it down. </p><br /></span>

He covers the usual gamet of Vonnegut trademarks - WWII, a reactive protagonist (as opposed to proactive) how art can be quite useless etc... The book is filled with great quotes and many thought provoking ideas. It's not one of his most famous books partially because (in my opinion) it has so many cross over themes from his other novels. He's talked about some of these themes before but comes at them from a different angle in Bluebeard. A very brilliant writer</span>
  <span id="freeText18429555166929364861" style="display:none">Another interesting Vonnegut book. He covers the usual gamet of Vonnegut trademarks - WWII, a reactive protagonist (as opposed to proactive) how art can be quite useless etc... The book is filled with great quotes and many thought provoking ideas. It's not one of his most famous books partially because (in my opinion) it has so many cross over themes from his other novels. He's talked about some of these themes before but comes at them from a different angle in Bluebeard. A very brilliant writer and one of my all time favorites. No one had his unique sense of humor and no one ever will. Kurt wondered when mankind will ever see the senseless of war. As he said, maybe it's time to give women a chance. </span>

<div id="review_1911749415" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1911749415" />
    <a title="Adrineh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8275804-adrineh"><img alt="Adrineh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334480859p2/8275804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1911749415?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adrineh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adrineh" href="/user/show/8275804-adrineh">Adrineh</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385295901" href="/book/show/532424.Bluebeard">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1911749415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1232796044226014977">By far, my favourite Vonnegut book (though, to be fair, I've only read two others: <i>Slaughterhouse-Five</i> and <i>Mother Night</i>). I'm also biased because the main character was Armenian, and I could relate. Incidentally, however, I was inspired to read Vonnegut from YouTuber climbthestacks. Her <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DRd-4M15iGU&t=365s' rel='nofollow noopener'>video series</a> on where to begin reading certain authors includes Vonnegut, and she too said her favourite book was <i>Bluebeard</i>, so I'm in good company :)<br/><br/>I read a library copy, but I'll definitely be buying this book</span>
  <span id="freeText1232796044226014977" style="display:none">By far, my favourite Vonnegut book (though, to be fair, I've only read two others: <i>Slaughterhouse-Five</i> and <i>Mother Night</i>). I'm also biased because the main character was Armenian, and I could relate. Incidentally, however, I was inspired to read Vonnegut from YouTuber climbthestacks. Her <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DRd-4M15iGU&amp;t=365s" rel="nofollow noopener">video series</a> on where to begin reading certain authors includes Vonnegut, and she too said her favourite book was <i>Bluebeard</i>, so I'm in good company :)<br /><br />I read a library copy, but I'll definitely be buying this book for my personal library.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1232796044226014977" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4825396966453081741">More Vonnegut...I really liked this one. Some of the smartest commentary on modern art (well, sort of modern -- the abstract expressionists) and just being human via art...Ah, I'm not doing this justice. It's grumpy and the ending is a little implausible (the final masterpiece sounds pretty cool the way a World War II diorama of infinite detail is cool...I like that sort of stuff, but I find it hard to consider it sublime, exactly). I miss Kurt Vonnegut. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3465232645" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3465232645" />
    <a title="Rachel Lu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25352562-rachel-lu"><img alt="Rachel Lu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585275426p2/25352562.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3465232645?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel Lu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel Lu" href="/user/show/25352562-rachel-lu">Rachel Lu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3465232645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15105490755924042459">One of my favorite teachers once told my class that Brothers K and Bluebeard were his two favorite novels of all time. To him, he said, great novels have been much more than entertainments or distractions. They’ve saved him, changed him, challenged him, awakened him. My only foray into Vonnegut then had been Slaughterhouse-Five. I knew little about Vonnegut’s oeuvre and even less about what his claim meant, but I was intrigued. Where and how did Bluebeard fit in his grandiose statement? At that </span>
  <span id="freeText15105490755924042459" style="display:none">One of my favorite teachers once told my class that Brothers K and Bluebeard were his two favorite novels of all time. To him, he said, great novels have been much more than entertainments or distractions. They’ve saved him, changed him, challenged him, awakened him. My only foray into Vonnegut then had been Slaughterhouse-Five. I knew little about Vonnegut’s oeuvre and even less about what his claim meant, but I was intrigued. Where and how did Bluebeard fit in his grandiose statement? At that time, I loved reading probably more than anything else, but the thought of a book “saving” me or even changing me was so far beyond my realm of experience that I couldn’t even begin to grasp the thought of it. But Bluebeard stayed tucked in the back of mind, often resurfacing between the quiet, contemplative lulls of finishing one novel and beginning the next. I promised myself I’d read it one day when the time was right, perhaps finally understand what he had meant. Three years later, I’ve finally picked it up. My good friend Gregory had told me he was planning on reading it this summer, and when’s a more perfect time than quarantine? Besides, I at last can say that novels have changed, challenged, awakened me and even—as Mr. Favorite Teacher claimed to a class of weary seniors who thought they knew it all—been great friends. So I read Bluebeard in snippets over the course of today, as I iced my post-wisdom teeth surgery mouth, popped various prescribed pills and watered my vegetable garden. Upon finishing it, I can’t claim that Bluebeard is one of my favorite novels or even my favorite Vonnegut, but I think I can understand why it might be someone else’s. <br /><br />Perhaps not as classically Vonnegut as his more renowned works, Bluebeard still retains his black humor, irony and absurdism, though perhaps seeped in more melancholy and introspection. Over and over, Vonnegut questions: what is art? What does it do that a shoe or potato cannot? What do people believe and why? Where is home? What is the burden you carry that you don’t let others see? Why do men do the things they do? What is the present and how do we live in it? What the fuck is Rabo Karabekian keeping locked in his potato barn?<br /><br />Bluebeard, Rabo tells us, is a fictitious character in folklore who upon marrying his latest child bride, tells her she can enter any room but one. Eventually, she, with the curiosity of her predecessors (Pandora &amp; Eve), succumbs to the forbidden temptation. Alas, Bluebeard catches her opening the door, which reveals the murdered bodies of Bluebeard’s former wives. So what can we do but wonder about the Rabo’s own inaccessible room and read on in hopes that we learn the answer? Rabo gives us a hint: what’s inside the potato barn is bigger than bread but smaller than Jupiter. It’s at once animal &amp; vegetable &amp; mineral. <br /><br />And now it is your turn, reader, to solve this riddle. Pick up this book, open it up and begin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15105490755924042459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6VgcA0HLQUkW8siq2LvzVCq5KAImMhLYEVDqJfJPvHRXI/1Kvvop3R8zk2S8VV/dewYez4fK9ng4j/s8RSED8g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sf5fMscdb1S/cSnpsD6O9/7Z+lCtSNbu0mT2sa3J454Phb57OCwHwLawcifU0CJ+r2bMnQywMk77u+eoGqdcGA==')})
  }

Yet the key to the origin of all these manifestations lies deep within our own brains. In The Origins of Psychic Phenomena, Stan Gooch explores the functioning of the dream-producing part of the brain--the cerebellum--and how the unconscious mind is able to externalize itself. The cerebellum is the physical seat of the unconscious and was once equal to or even superior to the cerebrum as essential to our functioning. In modern times it has been shunted into the subliminal, yet the cerebellum continues to process our worldly experiences and reveals its concerns in misunderstood, often frightening, manifestations. Gooch explains that Neanderthal Man possessed a much larger cerebellum than Cro-Magnon Man and posits that the modern repression of the cerebellum’s role in our consciousness has given rise to these           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2778055.Kurt_Vonnegut_Jr_"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1433582280p3/2778055.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7928696067236215039">Kurt Vonnegut, Junior was an American novelist, satirist, and most recently, graphic artist. He was recognized as New York State Author for 2001-2003. <br/><br/>He was born in Indianapolis, later the setting for many of his novels. He attended Cornell University from 1941 to 1943, where he wrote a column for the student newspaper, the Cornell Daily Sun. Vonnegut trained as a chemist and worked as a journali</span>
  <span id="freeText7928696067236215039" style="display:none">Kurt Vonnegut, Junior was an American novelist, satirist, and most recently, graphic artist. He was recognized as New York State Author for 2001-2003. <br /><br />He was born in Indianapolis, later the setting for many of his novels. He attended Cornell University from 1941 to 1943, where he wrote a column for the student newspaper, the Cornell Daily Sun. Vonnegut trained as a chemist and worked as a journalist before joining the U.S. Army and serving in World War II. <br /><br />After the war, he attended University of Chicago as a graduate student in anthropology and also worked as a police reporter at the City News Bureau of Chicago. He left Chicago to work in Schenectady, New York in public relations for General Electric. He attributed his unadorned writing style to his reporting work. <br /><br />His experiences as an advance scout in the Battle of the Bulge, and in particular his witnessing of the bombing of Dresden, Germany whilst a prisoner of war, would inform much of his work. This event would also form the core of his most famous work, Slaughterhouse-Five, the book which would make him a millionaire. This acerbic 200-page book is what most people mean when they describe a work as "Vonnegutian" in scope. <br /><br />Vonnegut was a self-proclaimed humanist and socialist (influenced by the style of Indiana's own Eugene V. Debs) and a lifelong supporter of the American Civil Liberties Union.<br /><br /><br />The novelist is known for works blending satire, black comedy and science fiction, such as Slaughterhouse-Five (1969), Cat's Cradle (1963), and Breakfast of Champions (1973) </span>
  <a data-text-id="7928696067236215039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
